  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app',

        state: 'apple_oauth_state_a8d38fb5-6ef0-491b-b563-9b5da1b992a9'
      });

When the gems are stolen and hidden in dark terrible places throughout the kingdom, the Shadow Lord triumphs, and Deltora is lost.<br /><br />In secrecy, with only a hand-drawn map to guide them, two unlikely companions set out on a perilous quest. Determined to find the lost gems and rid their land of the tyrant, they struggle towards their first goal - the sinister Forests of Silence.</span>

  <span id="freeText12364710619160603637" style="display:none">The Forests Of Silence (Deltora Quest, #1), Emily Rodda<br /><br /><b>The Forests of Silence</b> was written by <b>Emily Rodda</b>, and is the first book in the eight-volume <b>Deltora Quest</b> series. It was published in 2000 by Scholastic. <br /><br />The book opens with a boy called Jarred, a friend of Prince Endon. After the death of King Alton and his queen, Endon is proclaimed King in his father's place. <br /><br />To consummate this, a magical steel belt, the Belt of Deltora, is set around Endon's waist. The Belt recognizes Endon as Deltora's rightful king. Jarred goes to the library and learns that the evil Shadow Lord, a Sauron-like intelligence located in the Shadowlands, once tried to seize the land in which is the kingdom of Deltora. <br /><br />Because the people of those days were divided into seven tribes, the Shadow Army soon overwhelmed much of the land. Jarred learned that a blacksmith named Adin gathered the sacred talismans from each tribe and attached them to a chain of steel medallions. <br /><br />The people's trust in Adin, channeled through the gems, was powerful enough to drive back the Shadow Army into its own dark home, the Shadowlands. Adin later became king of the united land called Deltora; yet he never forgot that the Enemy was not destroyed. He therefore never let the Belt out of his sight. With every generation, the Belt was worn less and less, diminishing its effect. The kings and queens also let their power go to the administrative council, diminishing its power. ...<br /><br />تاریخ نخستین خوانش: روز نخست ماه مارس سال 2004میلادی<br /><br />عنوان: جنگلهای سکوت؛ نویسنده: امیلی رودا؛ مترجم محبوبه نجف خانی؛ تهران، قدیانی، 1382؛ در 159ص، مصور، نقشه؛ فروست در جستجوی دلتورا؛ رمان نوجوانان؛ شابک 97896417677؛ چاپ چهارم 1386؛ چاپ پنجم 1388؛ چاپ ششم 1389؛ موضوع: جادوگری - داستانهای کودکان و نوجوانان از نویسندگان امریکایی - سده 20م<br /><br />ارباب سایه ها تصمیم دارد، به شهر «دل» حمله کند، و تنها کمربند دلتورا هست که میتواند آنرا نجات میدهد.ارباب سایه ها، هفت گوهر کمربند را، در هفت مکان اهریمنی، و با نگهبانهایی شیطانی، مخفی کرده است.«آنا» و «جارد»، پسرشان «لیف» را، با همراه دیگری به نام «بارادا» به جستجوی گوهرهای کمربند میفرستند.«لیف» و «بارادا» برای پیدا کردن هر یک از گوهرهای کمربند، باید از روی نقشه ای کهن، به سوی محل اختفای گوهرها بروند، و آنها را به دست بیاورند.«جنگل‌های سکوت»، جلد نخست از سری هشت جلدی: «در جستجوی دلتورا»، اثر «امیلی رودا (جنیفر روو)» نویسنده «استرالیایی» است.؛ ا. شربیانی</span>

<div id="review_413562126" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/413562126" />
    <a title="Jason Sharp" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/12304786-jason-sharp"><img alt="Jason Sharp" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1346181490p2/12304786.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/413562126?book_show_action=true">Sep 13, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jason Sharp" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jason Sharp" href="/user/show/12304786-jason-sharp">Jason Sharp</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/12304786-jason-sharp?shelf=is571">is571</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer413562126" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16174058797391280002">This was one of my favorite book series as a teen and was the book that really got me into reading fantasy. It's technically considered a children's fiction book, but if you are interested in YA books don't let that scare you off as it's an excellent piece of fantasy writing. I recently read this book for the third of fourth time after a very long hiatus of reading hundreds of other YA and Adult fiction and I'm pleased to say that it holds up very well.<br/><br/>The series starts with a king and his best </span>
  <span id="freeText16174058797391280002" style="display:none">This was one of my favorite book series as a teen and was the book that really got me into reading fantasy. It's technically considered a children's fiction book, but if you are interested in YA books don't let that scare you off as it's an excellent piece of fantasy writing. I recently read this book for the third of fourth time after a very long hiatus of reading hundreds of other YA and Adult fiction and I'm pleased to say that it holds up very well.<br /><br />The series starts with a king and his best friend living a sheltered life. An evil (possibly demonic?) king appropriately named the Shadow Lord rules over the neighboring Shadowlands (see what they did there?) and has been trying to take over the land of Deltora for generations. The only protection that can keep away this evil king is a belt forged with 7 magical gems but when nefarious deeds cause the belt lose it's power it is destroyed and the gems are spread across the land each protected by an evil monstrous guardian. The Shadow Lord invades the country and a generation passes before the son of the king's best friend can take up the quest to find these lost gems. Each book details the travels of this teen and his battle against one of the guardians for a new gem.<br /><br />The first book is very interesting, but somewhat light on action as much of the story is being set-up. The books themselves are short, easy reads though and the amount of set-up is similar to what a much larger book would typically entail. If you have any interest in high fantasy writing full of magic, monsters and battles please give this a shot. It's available at many bookstores and libraries as a package where the first 4 volumes (out of 8) are collected together. The series has done so well that it has evolved from a standalone 8 book series to something much larger.<br /><br />Deltora Quest (8 books)<br />Deltora Shadowlands (3 books)<br />Dragons of Deltora (4 books)<br />There is also an anime based on the original series, a Japanese video game and a couple side books that flesh out the lore a bit (Book of Monsters, Art books, ect). <br /><br />It's an extremely popular series in Australia that's never quite taken off in America so if you can give it a chance. You will not regret it.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16174058797391280002" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer76353427647783642">My sister and I both read these in fourth grade (I graduated high school this May, for reference), and to this day they rank among our favorite series read, ever. And we both have read a LOT of books.<br/><br/>I forget the plots and the characters names and what even the major point of the quest was... but I do remember the fantastic time that I had reading these books. I remember running to the Childrens' section of Barnes and Noble and hoppping up and down waiting for my dad to buy us the next book, and</span>
  <span id="freeText76353427647783642" style="display:none">My sister and I both read these in fourth grade (I graduated high school this May, for reference), and to this day they rank among our favorite series read, ever. And we both have read a LOT of books.<br /><br />I forget the plots and the characters names and what even the major point of the quest was... but I do remember the fantastic time that I had reading these books. I remember running to the Childrens' section of Barnes and Noble and hoppping up and down waiting for my dad to buy us the next book, and physically fighting with my sister to read it first (this was before my dad set up the foolproof rotation system we later set in place to ensure peace in the household). I remember reading these books in class underneath my desk, completely engrossed. That is, until the teacher yelled at me for not paying attention to the lesson. I continued reading the next day anyway, the books were so damn good. <br /><br />I'm sure that if I went back now and read these books, they would lose their magic for me. They're intended entirely for children, and to rate them on an adult's scale is to do them a gross injustice.<br /><br />Let your child read the first book, and I can bet you you'll have a new and excited reader in the house. </span>
  <a data-text-id="76353427647783642" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2964876565?book_show_action=true">Sep 06, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Allyson (AllyEmReads)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Allyson (AllyEmReads)" href="/user/show/84161759-allyson-allyemreads">Allyson (AllyEmReads)</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2964876565" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9194270366348965301">I mean, it was a pretty solid beginning of a middle grade—no even younger—fantasy series. Emily Rodda was my and my sister’s favorite author when we were kids, around 7 or 8. I remember finding Deltora really scary, and I was absolutely obsessed with the world. I’m pretty sure I had all the extra books and even a few scholastic knickknacks centered around the series that you could get at the scholastic book fair. Anyone remember those? That was always the best week of elementary school. <br/><br/>Anyway, </span>
  <span id="freeText9194270366348965301" style="display:none">I mean, it was a pretty solid beginning of a middle grade—no even younger—fantasy series. Emily Rodda was my and my sister’s favorite author when we were kids, around 7 or 8. I remember finding Deltora really scary, and I was absolutely obsessed with the world. I’m pretty sure I had all the extra books and even a few scholastic knickknacks centered around the series that you could get at the scholastic book fair. Anyone remember those? That was always the best week of elementary school. <br /><br />Anyway, half the book was a prequel to set up the actual series, following the parents of the main character as they escaped the palace right before the fall of the empire and the Shadow Lord took over. Clearly this was juvenile but I still found it really entertaining. My only gripe is that apparently Emily Rodda hates using contractions?? There was literally not a single contraction in the entire book. It got old pretty fast but hey to each their own. <br /><br />I can’t remember all the books but I do remember that my favorite one was the one where they were going after the Opal simply because I love opals, being my birthstone and all. The monsters and mythology are so unique that I have to credit Rodda for that, well done indeed. I think it’ll be pretty fun to reread the rest of the series. </span>

<div id="review_3065679206" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3065679206" />
    <a title="Tanvi" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/62395472-tanvi"><img alt="Tanvi" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1574980659p2/62395472.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3065679206?book_show_action=true">Nov 28, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tanvi" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tanvi" href="/user/show/62395472-tanvi">Tanvi</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/20660837-the-forests-of-silence">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/62395472-tanvi?shelf=favourites">favourites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer3065679206&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3065679206" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9167853674474772048">The journey begins, and what a journey it is! Rodda doesn’t skimp on the action, the twists or the writing quality.<br/><br/>We open with a daring escape in the prologue. Then, in one of my favourite scenes in the entire series, we’re introduced to our hero, Lief, who’s stayed out after curfew. After pulling off another miraculous escape (a good example of Rodda’s trademark foreshadowing/repetition of scenes), he and an unlikely companion go on a quest to find the seven gems of the Belt.<br/><br/>The wonderful thin</span>
  <span id="freeText9167853674474772048" style="display:none">The journey begins, and what a journey it is! Rodda doesn’t skimp on the action, the twists or the writing quality.<br /><br />We open with a daring escape in the prologue. Then, in one of my favourite scenes in the entire series, we’re introduced to our hero, Lief, who’s stayed out after curfew. After pulling off another miraculous escape (a good example of Rodda’s trademark foreshadowing/repetition of scenes), he and an unlikely companion go on a quest to find the seven gems of the Belt.<br /><br />The wonderful thing about Lief (looking at you, Paolini) is that he really is an everyman, but he’s such an endearing one that it’s impossible not to like him. He feels very human, and that’s the most important thing. This book is really more of an introduction, but his character never really changes. If I had to name a weakness of these books, it would be that the character development really isn’t there, except maybe in Jasmine.<br /><br />The climax of The Forests of Silence isn’t as good as the next books, and its monster is very good, but not as compelling as some of the others. Jasmine shines here, but her best moments are in later books.<br /><br />That being said - this is how you write Newbery-worthy children’s lit, Rowling.<br /><br />To read Rodda is to read a master at work. No words are wasted. People and things mentioned in passing at the beginning of one book spring into prominence three chapters later, and not in the “I wrote this while drunk on sambuca” way.<br /><br />Do I sound like L. Ron Hubbard yet? This series is criminally underrated and it needs a publicity boost.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="9167853674474772048" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_502644672" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/502644672" />
    <a title="Michael Chen" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/16245175-michael-chen"><img alt="Michael Chen" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_50x66-632230dc9882b4352d753eedf9396530.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/502644672?book_show_action=true">Jan 10, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Michael Chen" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Michael Chen" href="/user/show/16245175-michael-chen">Michael Chen</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780439285995" href="/book/show/2277234.The_Forests_of_Silence">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer502644672" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9290947080866562971">I have just recently finished reading The Forests of Silence, by Emily Rodda. It the first book in the Deltora Quest books. I read this series when i was younger, and remembered that it was a nice escape from reality. After reading it again i can tell why i liked it so much as a kid.<br/>This book really isn’t like any other books I’ve read in recent memory. The best way Ii could describe it is like the Lord of the Rings, but much easier to read. The fantasy, creatures, and quest all remind me of tha</span>
  <span id="freeText9290947080866562971" style="display:none">I have just recently finished reading The Forests of Silence, by Emily Rodda. It the first book in the Deltora Quest books. I read this series when i was younger, and remembered that it was a nice escape from reality. After reading it again i can tell why i liked it so much as a kid.<br />This book really isn’t like any other books I’ve read in recent memory. The best way Ii could describe it is like the Lord of the Rings, but much easier to read. The fantasy, creatures, and quest all remind me of that series. The pure heart of Leif is equivalent to that of Frodo, and the Shadow Lord is the equivalent of the Dark Lord Sauron.<br />The way Rodda characterizes her characters is by good and evil, which is pretty straight forward. There’s practically no wiggle room in between. Leif is the main character of this book and is noble and courageous, but he is still young. He has much to learn, and as he matures, you feel as if your maturing with him. The Shadow Lord is Leif’s main problem and he sets out on a quest in order to try and rid Deltora of him by fixing the Belt of Deltora. To do this he must travel far and wide to find the gems that have been lost.<br />This conflict is rather deep and complex. The Shadow Lord ruling over Deltora, and Leif trying to get rid of him is an intense battle. Due to the origin of how the Shadow Lord came to rule Deltora, I felt more involved in the story than i would have if I had just been thrown into the plot as I am in most books.<br />As far as genre goes, I would categorize this book as action and adventure. The problems that Leif run into keep you on the edge of your seat throughout the book. I also enjoyed it because there were no sappy love stories, is was pure entertainment for me. The book ends on a massive cliffhanger that leaves the reader practically drooling to get their hands on the next book in the series to see what happens to Leif throughout his quest.<br />I awarded this book 4 stars because I felt is is very entertaining. Although it is not chalked full of literary genius, the riddles expertly woven throughout the book and the plot and characters do its job in entertainment. I would recommend this book if you enjoy adventure, and you’re looking for a relatively quick, entertaining read.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9290947080866562971" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3491879898" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3491879898" />
    <a title="Nathan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/44601235-nathan"><img alt="Nathan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1597240935p2/44601235.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3491879898?book_show_action=true">Aug 13, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nathan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nathan" href="/user/show/44601235-nathan">Nathan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3491879898" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5169761014665799823">I always wanted to read these books as a kid. I saw them at book fairs, in the library and in Barnes and Noble all the time. But for some reason, I never picked them up. I think not reading these books as a kid may be one of my greatest literary regrets in life, because I know that as an adult, I'll never be able to enjoy them as much as I probably would have at age 10. <br/><br/>...Right?<br/><br/>Yeah, that's probably right. I breezed through this pretty fast, and although I enjoyed it, I can definitely see that </span>
  <span id="freeText5169761014665799823" style="display:none">I always wanted to read these books as a kid. I saw them at book fairs, in the library and in Barnes and Noble all the time. But for some reason, I never picked them up. I think not reading these books as a kid may be one of my greatest literary regrets in life, because I know that as an adult, I'll never be able to enjoy them as much as I probably would have at age 10. <br /><br />...Right?<br /><br />Yeah, that's probably right. I breezed through this pretty fast, and although I enjoyed it, I can definitely see that there is stuff that sticks out to me as an adult, (And as an amateur writer,) that I never would have noticed as a kid.<br /><br /><i>The Forests of Silence</i> is basically a prologue to set up the rest of the series, and the story only really gets going a little over halfway into the book. By the end of it, we've finally collected all our characters and we're ready to start on our quest, after facing down the knight you see on the cover. (I always thought this one had the coolest cover. A sweet holographic bronze knight, how awesome is that?) Like I said, coming through this as an adult means you're going to notice occasional over-explanation of stuff that you could've seen coming a mile away, hackneyed plot devices and simplistic story elements/thematic content. (Like... The nebulous villain is just "The Shadow Lord." He's... evil!) <br /><br />But you know what? Whatever. I'm going to keep going. As of right now, I don't feel like the world of Deltora has much life in it, but I'm only one book in. I'll keep you posted as I go to see if my opinion changes. But as of the end of book one, I feel like you definitely have to be a kid to really enjoy these books. (Or you have to have grown up reading them, and then come back to them as an adult.) We'll see if subsequent books will change my mind.<br /><br />Oh, one more thing. There is one thing that can't be denied so far about these books; they're packed with imagination. I can just imagine myself (or any other kid of the target age,) drawing my own versions of the monsters and characters from this book and being inspired to write my own stories. <br /><br />More thoughts to come with the next book.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5169761014665799823" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText16475131043805570849" style="display:none">Deltora Quest is a series for children, but the world that is created by the skillful mind of Emily Rodda will stay with you. The characters will remain your friends, and the words will be there to entertain, comfort, distract, anytime you choose to pick up any one of the 1st, 2nd or 3rd Deltora Quest series. My love for these books and my gratitude towards Emily Rodda for creating them cannot be accurately explained without sounding creepy. Which is unfortunate. <br />Deltora became a home away from home for me. Simple, easy to read, not hampered by whiney characters consumed in their undying love for each other, a true adventure series. If you miss the imaginary worlds you created as a child and wish, for a couple of hours, to slip back into one, then pick up this book and begin your Deltora Quest. You'll be set.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16475131043805570849" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3240853935" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3240853935" />
    <a title="Jaeger Nelson" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/111579555-jaeger-nelson"><img alt="Jaeger Nelson" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_50x66-632230dc9882b4352d753eedf9396530.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3240853935?book_show_action=true">May 14, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jaeger Nelson" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jaeger Nelson" href="/user/show/111579555-jaeger-nelson">Jaeger Nelson</a>
      </span>

          added it




        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3240853935" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13851848728339912023">To start off here is a little introduction to the book, This is one of your classic fantasy books. It starts off with a young boy and he suddenly finds himself being given a quest and he sets off on a massive journey to achieve his goal and eventually save the day. This is very similar to books like Michael Vey, or Ranger’s Apprentice. For starters the first literary element we are going to be talking about is foreshadowing. Foreshadowing is when you hint to an outcome before it is fully reveale</span>
  <span id="freeText13851848728339912023" style="display:none">To start off here is a little introduction to the book, This is one of your classic fantasy books. It starts off with a young boy and he suddenly finds himself being given a quest and he sets off on a massive journey to achieve his goal and eventually save the day. This is very similar to books like Michael Vey, or Ranger’s Apprentice. For starters the first literary element we are going to be talking about is foreshadowing. Foreshadowing is when you hint to an outcome before it is fully revealed, so you kind of get a taste of what’s to come. For example,”“The king had died of the mysterious fever that had kept him to his bed for the last few weeks. King Alton was dead like his wife- the fever had taken them both.” In this example, The foreshadowing is in the word choice. She decides to call it a mysterious fever instead of just a common sickness. Then we also learn that his wife was taken by this strange fever not too long before him. So what are we all automatically thinking? My mind jumped right to poisoning as soon as I read that. As we come to discover later, this was indeed the case in how the king and queen died. Secondly the next literary element we will be discussing is flashbacks. This is when you are in the present day and the author takes you to the past to divulge more information and tell you a little bit more about your characters. For instance, ““Together they did their lessons in the school room- they had a secret hiding place- they practiced archery together- they invented a secret code and used it to pass messages.” Now this isn’t the exact quote since the flashback was a few pages long but I pieced the most important parts together. Now what this quote tells us in the story is now that the king is dead it’s up to Endon to take up his father's mantle. This signifies the end of being a child for him. So the author takes us on a little trip into his childhood growing up with Jared and all the fun things they would do. This is a very good flashback because it shows us how good of a bond Jared and Edmond had, they literally did everything together and were basically inseparable. Now to rap it all up I would like to give my recommendation. I would definitely recommend this book to anyone who likes a good action adventure/fantasy book. If you like books such as Lord of the Rings or Micheal Vey, then you will love this series. It has very good writing and storytelling and will keep you captivated for hours if you let it.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13851848728339912023" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_432556118" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/432556118" />
    <a title="Harold Ogle" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/198484-harold-ogle"><img alt="Harold Ogle" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1317340769p2/198484.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/432556118?book_show_action=true">Oct 11, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Harold Ogle" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Harold Ogle" href="/user/show/198484-harold-ogle">Harold Ogle</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/198484-harold-ogle?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/198484-harold-ogle?shelf=magic">magic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/198484-harold-ogle?shelf=quest">quest</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer432556118" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10533876351142078263">Based on this book, "Deltora Quest" seems to be a great transition series for the move from children's books to YA books. This first book in the first series (apparently the Deltora line resembles the "Beast Quest" line, in that they are both long series of books broken up into shorter "mini-series" of five or six volumes each) has an unusually complicated plot (for a children's book), and protagonists that exhibit a more complicated emotional range than most children's books (they're angry, con</span>
  <span id="freeText10533876351142078263" style="display:none">Based on this book, "Deltora Quest" seems to be a great transition series for the move from children's books to YA books. This first book in the first series (apparently the Deltora line resembles the "Beast Quest" line, in that they are both long series of books broken up into shorter "mini-series" of five or six volumes each) has an unusually complicated plot (for a children's book), and protagonists that exhibit a more complicated emotional range than most children's books (they're angry, confused, and, at times, both resentful and distrustful of each other). This relative complexity of plot and character is typical of YA literature (in addition, several of the protagonists are orphans, which, as we know, is instant shorthand for all YA literature everywhere), but the speed with which the protagonist is thrust into and accepts the adventure is typical of children's stories. There are also some YA themes - the main protagonist muses on death and futility in at least two places - which really earmark this book as for pre-teens. A lot of this stuff is given what feels like short shrift, however: the pacing is very much like a children's series in that the plot doesn't let up for long enough at any point to expand on any of the deeper issues. For instance, the climax of the action takes place with a Gollum-like revenant knight who is obsessed with living forever. Pretty rich soil for a deep back story, which turns out to be a teaser, because it is discarded when he is defeated.<br /><br />I enjoyed this book for its relative complexity and the intriguing glimpses of compelling character stories.<br /><br />Here's the review:<a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[<br />Deltora is a kingdom in a magical land. Once, it was composed of seven warring tribes/clans/regions, each with their own totemic artifact that blessed and protected the tribe. Then the Shadow Lord invaded and began to conquer. Divided, the tribes could not resist the Shadow Lord's depredations, even with their Gems of Power. But one man, a humble blacksmith, forged an alliance between all the tribes, just as he forged a mighty Belt that would bear and amplify the power of all seven gems. The united country was able to defeat the Shadow Lord, driving him away with the belt-clad blacksmith leading a mighty host. The blacksmith was crowned first king of Deltora. His line has continued unbroken for many generations, but each subsequent generation has felt the need to maintain vigilance less and less, and so the belt has been largely forgotten except in official state functions/ceremonies. The book begins with the death of the current king, and his son the young prince's ascension to the throne. His best friend, an orphan of a noble who has been raised in the court, wonders why the Belt only appears at the coronation. He investigates and uncovers a plot by the seneschal to overthrow the kingdom and pave the way for the return of the Shadow Lord. He tries to convince his friend, the King, but the seneschal returns then and turns the King against him. The boy flees the castle (which has been enchanted to always present a picture of prosperity to anyone within), accused of murder, and finds his way through the destitute countryside to the local smithy (which may or may not have been the remains of the original king's smithy), where he is welcomed and sheltered. Many years pass, and both the boy and the King grow to adulthood, marry and have children. The boy takes over as village blacksmith. Finally, the King realizes that his friend may have been correct, and secretly sends for his friend to return. The blacksmith sneaks back into the enchanted castle, convinces the King and Queen to retrieve the Belt, but, sadly, after the Belt has been stolen and sundered into its component gems. Agents of the Shadow Lord, loath to touch the gems and unable to destroy them, have scattered them across the land and appointed fell guardians to prevent their retrieval. The Queen kills the seneschal - the only witness to their discovering the truth - and they all flee the palace to cover their tracks. <br /><br />That's all prologue, effectively. The rest of the story takes place sixteen years later, when the child of the blacksmith is nearly fully-grown. The Shadow Lord has taken control of the kingdom in the meantime, ruling the land through the dominance of his Grey Guards, a ruthless army of thugs and brigands. The Shadow Lord has grown complacent in his rule, however, and started to turn his attention of further conquest. He recalls most of the Grey Guards to campaign in another country, and so the blacksmith reveals the truth to his son. Due to an accident some years before, the smith himself is unable to pursue the quest of retrieving the gems, but he has salvaged the Belt itself. He sends his son in his stead: find the gems, reassemble the belt, and we'll get the king out of hiding to wield it in defeating the Shadow Lord. Accompanying the son is the grown son of one of the castle servants, an orphan who was sheltered by the blacksmith and his wife. He's a strong and bold warrior with a penchant for deceit and stealth, as he's been hiding his true identity under the guise of an idiot beggar for at least a decade. He resents that the son must come along, as he feels that he should be able to do the quest on his own. But the blacksmith insists, and the warrior grudgingly relents. <br /><br />They set off under cover of darkness, evading sparse Grey Guard patrols, and make their way to the Forests of Silence. There, they are immediately set upon by fell wights who paralyze them and carry them off to feed their "god:" a grotesque abomination straight out of a Robert E. Howard Conan story. They manage to escape with the help of a wild orphan girl who takes pity on them, and they flee through the tree-tops. They bond with the girl a little bit, though they both think she's a bit touched in the head (she keeps referring to how she has conversations with the trees and woodland animals), then continue on their quest. At the center of the innermost woods of the Forests of Silence, they meet the revenant of a knight who, long before the tribes were united to fight the Shadow Lord, came to covet the Lilies of Light. The Lilies produce a nectar of healing and, potentially, immortality. The Knight has kept himself going for thousands of years by his avaricious guarding of the Lilies. He's grown choking vines that smother that part of the forest, cultivating them on the blood of any creature, sentient or non-, that enters his reach. After the boy and the warrior fight him to a standstill and are surely doomed - the warrior is dealt a mortal blow and his blood also soaks the roots of the vines - the girl convinces a living tree to crack off a huge bough to crush the knight into oblivion. They retrieve the gem, use the Lilies to heal the warrior, and determine to continue on to find the next Gem. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span></span>
  <a data-text-id="10533876351142078263" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText1495755499302085780" style="display:none">Wow, very detailed and action packed started to a series. I loved it when we were told that Jarred and Endon had a secret code as a kid in order to tell each other if Min was near. I kinda got sad and frustrated at the same time when Endon is crowned as king and is forced out of his friendship with Jarred. Prandine is very forceful, and seems to hate Jarred. I'm surprised Lief agreed to accepting the quest with his companion, Barda the 'beggar'. They actually made a friend, Jasmine who has two pets, and managed to get the first gem, the topaz.       <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Gianni" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Gianni" href="/user/show/85492761-gianni">Gianni</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/85492761-gianni?shelf=deltora-quest">deltora-quest</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2496560512" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17570062961105558487">Emily Rodda's book "Deltora Quest; The Forest of Silence" is a fantasy book with magic like no other. The Kingdom of Deltora is under attack from the Shadow Lord and our heroes must find the 7 magic gems to eliminate the Shadow Lord and stop his Evil doings. Now, will our win the battle or succumb to the Evil the Shadow Lord is raining across the Kingdom of Deltora.<br/><br/>Rodda uses Character, Setting, and Theme well to have the story be delightful to read. By having the setting in a castle and a creep</span>
  <span id="freeText17570062961105558487" style="display:none">Emily Rodda's book "Deltora Quest; The Forest of Silence" is a fantasy book with magic like no other. The Kingdom of Deltora is under attack from the Shadow Lord and our heroes must find the 7 magic gems to eliminate the Shadow Lord and stop his Evil doings. Now, will our win the battle or succumb to the Evil the Shadow Lord is raining across the Kingdom of Deltora.<br /><br />Rodda uses Character, Setting, and Theme well to have the story be delightful to read. By having the setting in a castle and a creepy forest helps push the magical ambiance in the story. The setting also helps character by having apart when a girl jumps out of a tree and join the heroes in there quest. And the theme the author gives is that good always prevail over evil. These elements of fiction help drive the story further keeping it interesting.<br /> <br />In my opinion, the book was really well put out and has a potential for an enjoyable series. But it also has a slight pacing issue when the heroes are walking towards the forest it seemed fast and I think it could have been a bit longer to make the books pacing a bit better but other than that the book was very good. I would rate this book an 8 out of 10 because I enjoyed the story and hope the rest of the books in the series are just as good. I would recommend this book to kids and adults who love magic and fantasy stories.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17570062961105558487" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2117614352" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10534794067297059311">I adored this series as a kid, and re-reading it now, it actually still stands up. Is is simple? Yes. Are there large chunks of exposition? Yes. Does the whole books feel like a rip off of the Lord of the Rings? Absolutely! But it's engaging and descriptive without being flowery or on the nose, female characters are treated the same as any other, and the plot flows swiftly. For a kid's adventure book, this thing ticks all the boxes for me.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3594568981" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer910042590731859556">I really enjoyed this book. It is an interesting adventure book that has adventurous characters. The goal of the book is to find a missing gems and return them to the stone. My favorite part is when the characters head into the forest of silence to find the gem without being spotted by the guardian.<br/><br/>Thanks guys have a good one!</span>

          </span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('bqKgs+ROd23VQhtf1zE7aLc36KXfAP7dastcxao6RmwwlTrSBYlpZkWrW+UHIbT/6xGCm40RD1TmQLPsyX4RYQ==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('mCwIJ4SZ3bA8y+/qWcGuBtFiW2lFsOZ5PyB8I3AB8+/GG5JGZV7Du6wir1CJ0SGRjUQxVxehF/Czq5MKE0Wk4g==')})
  }

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer6182635747981143829">Emily Rodda (real name Jennifer Rowe) was born in Sydney, Australia and graduated from the University of Sydney in 1973 with an MA (Hons) in English Literature. Moving into a publishing career, first as a book editor, and finally as a publisher at Angus & Robertson, Rodda's first book was published in 1984.</span>
  <span id="freeText6182635747981143829" style="display:none">Emily Rodda (real name Jennifer Rowe) was born in Sydney, Australia and graduated from the University of Sydney in 1973 with an MA (Hons) in English Literature. Moving into a publishing career, first as a book editor, and finally as a publisher at Angus &amp; Robertson, Rodda's first book was published in 1984.</span>
